.navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Template:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Template talk:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TopicTOC-Biology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p><b>Molecular biology</b> <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> that seeks to understand the <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecular</a> basis of biological activity in and between <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecular</a> synthesis, modification, mechanisms, and interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cell2_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-cell2-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The study of chemical and physical structure of biological macromolecules is known as molecular biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Molecular biology was first described as an approach focused on the underpinnings of biological phenomena - uncovering the structures of biological molecules as well as their interactions, and how these interactions explain observations of classical biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1945 the term molecular biology was used by physicist William Astbury. In 1953 Francis Crick, James Watson, and colleagues, working at Medical Research Council unit, Cavendish laboratory, Cambridge (now the <a href="/wiki/MRC_Laboratory_of_Molecular_Biology" title="MRC Laboratory of Molecular Biology">MRC Laboratory of Molecular Biology</a>), made a double helix model of DNA which changed the entire research scenario. They proposed the DNA structure based on previous research done by Rosalind Franklin and Maurice Wilkins. This research then lead to finding DNA material in other microorganisms, plants and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Molecular biology is not simply the study of biological molecules and their interactions; rather, it is also a collection of techniques developed since the field's genesis which have enabled scientists to learn about molecular processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">[6]</a></sup> In this way it has both complemented and improved <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> as methods (of understanding <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>) that began before its advent. One notable technique which has revolutionized the field is the polymerase chain reaction (PCR), which was developed in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">[6]</a></sup> PCR is a reaction which amplifies small quantities of DNA, and it is used in many applications across scientific disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_dogma_of_molecular_biology" title="Central dogma of molecular biology">central dogma of molecular biology</a> describes the process in which DNA is transcribed into RNA, which is then translated into protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Molecular biology also plays a critical role in the understanding of structures, functions, and internal controls within individual <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, all of which can be used to efficiently target new <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drugs</a>, diagnose disease, and better understand cell physiology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Some clinical research and medical therapies arising from molecular biology are covered under <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a> whereas the use of molecular biology or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_cell_biology" title="Molecular cell biology">molecular cell biology</a> in medicine is now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_medicine" title="Molecular medicine">molecular medicine</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_molecular_biology">History of molecular biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of molecular biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_molecular_biology" title="History of molecular biology">History of molecular biology</a></div>
<p>Molecular biology sits at the intersection of biochemistry and genetics; as these scientific disciplines emerged and evolved in the 20th century, it became clear that they both sought to determine the molecular mechanisms which underlie vital cellular functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Advances in molecular biology have been closely related to the development of new technologies and their optimization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Molecular biology has been elucidated by the work of many scientists, and thus the history of the field depends on an understanding of these scientists and their experiments.
</p><p>The field of genetics arose as an attempt to understand the molecular mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">genetic inheritance</a> and the structure of a <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gregor_Mendel" title="Gregor Mendel">Gregor Mendel</a> pioneered this work in 1866, when he first wrote the laws of genetic inheritance based on his studies of mating crosses in pea plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> One such law of genetic inheritance is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_Segregation" title="Law of Segregation">law of segregation</a>, which states that diploid individuals with two <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> for a particular gene will pass one of these alleles to their offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Because of his critical work, the study of genetic inheritance is commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian genetics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major milestone in molecular biology was the discovery of the structure of DNA. This work began in 1869 by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Miescher" title="Friedrich Miescher">Friedrich Miescher</a>, a Swiss biochemist who first proposed a structure called <i>nuclein</i>, which we now know to be deoxyribonucleic acid, or DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-16">[16]</a></sup> He discovered this unique substance by studying the components of pus-filled bandages, and noting the unique properties of the "phosphorus-containing substances".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Another notable contributor to the DNA model was <a href="/wiki/Phoebus_Levene" title="Phoebus Levene">Phoebus Levene</a>, who proposed the "polynucleotide model" of DNA in 1919 as a result of his biochemical experiments on yeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1950, <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Chargaff" title="Erwin Chargaff">Erwin Chargaff</a> expanded on the work of Levene and elucidated a few critical properties of nucleic acids: first, the sequence of nucleic acids varies across species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Second, the total concentration of purines (adenine and guanine) is always equal to the total concentration of pyrimidines (cysteine and thymine).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-16">[16]</a></sup> This is now known as Chargaff's rule. In 1953, <a href="/wiki/James_Watson" title="James Watson">James Watson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> published the double helical structure of DNA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-20">[20]</a></sup> using the <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> work done by <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Wilkins" title="Maurice Wilkins">Maurice Wilkins</a>. Watson and Crick described the structure of DNA and conjectured about the implications of this unique structure for possible mechanisms of DNA replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>J. D. Watson and F. H. C. Crick were awarded Nobel prize in 1962, along with Maurice Wilkens, for proposing a model of the structure of DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-:9-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1961, it was demonstrated that when a <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> encodes a <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, three sequential bases of a gene's <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> specify each successive amino acid of the protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>  Thus the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> is a triplet code, where each triplet (called a codon) specifies a particular amino acid.  Furthermore, it was shown that the codons do not overlap with each other in the DNA sequence encoding a protein, and that each sequence is read from a fixed starting point.
</p><p>
During 1962–1964, through the use of conditional lethal mutants of a bacterial virus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> fundamental advances were made in our understanding of the functions and interactions of the proteins employed in the machinery of <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Genetic_recombination" title="Genetic recombination">DNA recombination</a>, and in the assembly of molecular structures.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="3075" decoding="async" height="351" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png/360px-DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png/540px-DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png/720px-DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DNA_Structure%2BKey%2BLabelled.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagrammatic representation of Watson and Crick's DNA structure</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:364px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="1002" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png/362px-Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png/543px-Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png/724px-Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png 2x" width="362"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hendiduras_mayor_menor-eo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Angle description in DNA structure</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_F.Griffith_experiment">The F.Griffith experiment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The F.Griffith experiment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:01_dna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1512" data-file-width="2738" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/01_dna.jpg/360px-01_dna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/01_dna.jpg/540px-01_dna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/01_dna.jpg/720px-01_dna.jpg 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:01_dna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagrammatic representation of experiment</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Griffith%27s_experiment" title="Griffith's experiment">Griffith's experiment</a></div>
<p>In 1928, Fredrick Griffith, encountered a virulence property in pneumococcus bacteria, which was killing lab rats. According to Mendel, prevalent at that time, gene transfer could occur only from parent to daughter cells only. Griffith advanced another theory, stating that gene transfer occurring in member of same generation is known as horizontal gene transfer (HGT). This phenomenon is now referred to as genetic transformation.
</p><p>Griffith addressed the <i>Streptococcus pneumoniae</i> bacteria, which had two different strains, one virulent and smooth and one avirulent and rough. The smooth strain had glistering appearance owing to the presence of a type of specific polysaccharide – a polymer of glucose and glucuronic acid capsule. Due to this polysaccharide layer of bacteria, a host's immune system cannot recognize the bacteria and it kills the host. The other, avirulent, rough strain lacks this polysaccharide capsule and has a dull, rough appearance.
</p><p>Presence or absence of capsule in the  strain, is known to be genetically determined. Smooth and rough strains occur in several different type such as S-I, S-II, S-III, etc. and R-I, R-II, R-III, etc. respectively. All this subtypes of S and R bacteria differ with each other in antigen type they produce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-:9-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hershey_and_Chase_experiment">Hershey and Chase experiment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hershey and Chase experiment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hershey%E2%80%93Chase_experiment" title="Hershey–Chase experiment">Hershey–Chase experiment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hershey_Chase_experiment.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="851" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hershey_Chase_experiment.png/310px-Hershey_Chase_experiment.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hershey_Chase_experiment.png/465px-Hershey_Chase_experiment.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hershey_Chase_experiment.png/620px-Hershey_Chase_experiment.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hershey_Chase_experiment.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hershey and Chase experiment</div></div></div>
<p>Confirmation that DNA is the genetic material which is cause of infection came from Hershey and Chase experiment. They used <i>E.coli</i> and bacteriophage for the experiment. This experiment is also known as blender experiment, as kitchen blender was used as a major piece of apparatus. <i>Alfred Hershey</i> and <i>Martha Chase</i> demonstrated that the DNA injected by a phage particle into a bacterium contains all information required to synthesize progeny phage particles. They used radioactivity to tag the bacteriophage's protein coat with radioactive sulphur and DNA with radioactive phosphorus, into two different test tubes respectively. After mixing bacteriophage and <i>E.coli</i> into the test tube, the incubation period starts in which phage transforms the genetic material in the <i>E.coli</i> cells. Then the mixture is blended or agitated, which separates the phage from <i>E.coli</i> cells. The whole mixture is centrifuged and the pellet which contains <i>E.coli</i> cells was checked and the supernatant was discarded. The <i>E.coli</i> cells showed radioactive phosphorus, which indicated that the transformed material was DNA not the protein coat.
</p><p>The transformed DNA gets attached to the DNA of <i>E.coli</i> and radioactivity is only seen onto the bacteriophage's DNA. This mutated DNA can be passed to the next generation and the theory of Transduction came into existence. Transduction is a process in which the bacterial DNA carry the fragment of bacteriophages and pass it on the next generation. This is also a type of horizontal gene transfer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-:9-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_molecular_biology">Modern molecular biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern molecular biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the mid-2020s, molecular biology entered a golden age defined by both vertical and horizontal technical development. Vertically, novel technologies are allowing for real-time monitoring of biological processes at the atomic level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Molecular biologists today have access to increasingly affordable sequencing data at increasingly higher depths, facilitating the development of novel genetic manipulation methods in new non-model organisms. Likewise, synthetic molecular biologists will drive the industrial production of small and macro molecules through the introduction of exogenous metabolic pathways in various prokaryotic and eukaryotic cell lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horizontally, sequencing data is becoming more affordable and utilized in many different scientific fields. This will drive the development of industries in developing nations and increase accessibility to individual researchers. Likewise, CRISPR-Cas9 gene editing experiments can now be conceived and implemented by individuals for under $10,000 in novel organisms, which will drive the development of industrial and medical applications <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_other_biological_sciences">Relationship to other biological sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Relationship to other biological sciences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry,_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="875" data-file-width="906" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg/220px-Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg/330px-Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg/440px-Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry,_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic relationship between <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> and molecular biology</div></div></div>
<p>The following list describes a viewpoint on the interdisciplinary relationships between molecular biology and other related fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><b>Molecular biology</b></i> is the study of the molecular underpinnings of the biological phenomena, focusing on molecular synthesis, modification, mechanisms and interactions.</li>
<li><i><b>Biochemistry</b></i> is the study of the chemical substances and vital processes occurring in living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>. <a href="/wiki/Biochemist" title="Biochemist">Biochemists</a> focus heavily on the role, function, and structure of <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b>Genetics</b></i> is the study of how genetic differences affect organisms. <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">Genetics</a> attempts to predict how <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a>, individual <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epistasis" title="Epistasis">genetic interactions</a> can affect the expression of a <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>While researchers practice techniques specific to molecular biology, it is common to combine these with methods from <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>. Much of molecular biology is quantitative, and recently a significant amount of work has been done using computer science techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_biology" title="Computational biology">computational biology</a>. <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">Molecular genetics</a>, the study of gene structure and function, has been among the most prominent sub-fields of molecular biology since the early 2000s. Other branches of biology are informed by molecular biology, by either directly studying the interactions of molecules in their own right such as in <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cell biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a>, or indirectly, where molecular techniques are used to infer historical attributes of <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">populations</a> or <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, as in fields in <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics" title="Population genetics">population genetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetics</a>. There is also a long tradition of studying <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> "from the ground up", or molecularly, in <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tian_2013_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tian_2013-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Techniques_of_molecular_biology">Techniques of molecular biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Techniques of molecular biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:183px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DNA_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="181" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/DNA_animation.gif" width="181"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DNA_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>DNA animation</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more extensive list on protein methods, see <a href="/wiki/Protein_methods" title="Protein methods">protein methods</a>. For more extensive list on nucleic acid methods, see <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_methods" title="Nucleic acid methods">nucleic acid methods</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_cloning">Molecular cloning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Molecular cloning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloning" title="Molecular cloning">Molecular cloning</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transduction_image.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Transduction_image.pdf/page1-220px-Transduction_image.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Transduction_image.pdf/page1-330px-Transduction_image.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Transduction_image.pdf/page1-440px-Transduction_image.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transduction_image.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transduction image</div></div></div>
<p>Molecular cloning is used to isolate and then transfer a DNA sequence of interest into a plasmid vector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> This recombinant DNA technology was first developed in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In this technique, a <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> sequence coding for a protein of interest is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clone_(genetics)" title="Clone (genetics)">cloned</a> using <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> (PCR), and/or <a href="/wiki/Restriction_enzyme" title="Restriction enzyme">restriction enzymes</a>, into a <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> (<a href="/wiki/Expression_vector" title="Expression vector">expression vector</a>). The plasmid vector usually has at least 3 distinctive features: an origin of replication, a <a href="/wiki/Multiple_cloning_site" title="Multiple cloning site">multiple cloning site</a> (MCS), and a selective marker (usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>). Additionally, upstream of the MCS are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Promoter_region" title="Promoter region">promoter regions</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a> start site, which regulate the expression of cloned gene.
</p><p>This plasmid can be inserted into either bacterial or animal cells. Introducing DNA into bacterial cells can be done by <a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)" title="Transformation (genetics)">transformation</a> via uptake of naked DNA, <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_conjugation" title="Bacterial conjugation">conjugation</a> via cell-cell contact or by <a href="/wiki/Transduction_(genetics)" title="Transduction (genetics)">transduction</a> via viral vector. Introducing DNA into <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> cells, such as animal cells, by physical or chemical means is called <a href="/wiki/Transfection" title="Transfection">transfection</a>. Several different transfection techniques are available, such as calcium phosphate transfection, <a href="/wiki/Electroporation" title="Electroporation">electroporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microinjection" title="Microinjection">microinjection</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liposome_transfection" title="Liposome transfection">liposome transfection</a>. The plasmid may be integrated into the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>, resulting in a stable transfection, or may remain independent of the genome and expressed temporarily, called a transient transfection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cell_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-cell-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>DNA coding for a protein of interest is now inside a cell, and the <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> can now be expressed. A variety of systems, such as inducible promoters and specific cell-signaling factors, are available to help express the protein of interest at high levels. Large quantities of a protein can then be extracted from the bacterial or eukaryotic cell. The protein can be tested for enzymatic activity under a variety of situations, the protein may be crystallized so its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tertiary_structure" title="Tertiary structure">tertiary structure</a> can be studied, or, in the pharmaceutical industry, the activity of new drugs against the protein can be studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polymerase_chain_reaction">Polymerase chain reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Polymerase chain reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">Polymerase chain reaction</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">Polymerase chain reaction</a> (PCR) is an extremely versatile technique for copying DNA. In brief, PCR allows a specific <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA sequence</a> to be copied or modified in predetermined ways. The reaction is extremely powerful and under perfect conditions could amplify one DNA molecule to become 1.07 billion molecules in less than two hours. PCR has many applications, including the study of gene expression, the detection of pathogenic microorganisms, the detection of genetic mutations, and the introduction of mutations to DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The PCR technique can be used to introduce <a href="/wiki/Restriction_site" title="Restriction site">restriction enzyme sites</a> to ends of DNA molecules, or to mutate particular bases of DNA, the latter is a method referred to as <a href="/wiki/Site-directed_mutagenesis" title="Site-directed mutagenesis">site-directed mutagenesis</a>. PCR can also be used to determine whether a particular DNA fragment is found in a <a href="/wiki/CDNA_library" title="CDNA library">cDNA library</a>. PCR has many variations, like reverse transcription PCR (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RT-PCR" title="RT-PCR">RT-PCR</a>) for amplification of RNA, and, more recently, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_PCR" title="Quantitative PCR">quantitative PCR</a> which allow for quantitative measurement of DNA or RNA molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_(Front,_angled).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_%28Front%2C_angled%29.jpg/220px-Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_%28Front%2C_angled%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_%28Front%2C_angled%29.jpg/330px-Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_%28Front%2C_angled%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_%28Front%2C_angled%29.jpg/440px-Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_%28Front%2C_angled%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_percent_Agarose_Gel_in_Borate_Buffer_cast_in_a_Gel_Tray_(Front,_angled).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two percent <a href="/wiki/Agarose" title="Agarose">agarose gel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Borate_buffered_saline" title="Borate buffered saline">borate buffer cast</a> in a gel tray.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gel_electrophoresis">Gel electrophoresis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gel electrophoresis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SDS-PAGE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="289" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/SDS-PAGE.jpg/125px-SDS-PAGE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/SDS-PAGE.jpg/188px-SDS-PAGE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/SDS-PAGE.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SDS-PAGE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SDS-PAGE</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gel_electrophoresis" title="Gel electrophoresis">Gel electrophoresis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gel_electrophoresis" title="Gel electrophoresis">Gel electrophoresis</a> is a technique which separates molecules by their size using an agarose or polyacrylamide gel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-38">[38]</a></sup> This technique is one of the principal tools of molecular biology. The basic principle is that DNA fragments can be separated by applying an electric current across the gel - because the DNA backbone contains negatively charged phosphate groups, the DNA will migrate through the agarose gel towards the positive end of the current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-38">[38]</a></sup> Proteins can also be separated on the basis of size using an <a href="/wiki/SDS-PAGE" title="SDS-PAGE">SDS-PAGE</a> gel, or on the basis of size and their <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> by using what is known as a <a href="/wiki/Two-dimensional_gel_electrophoresis" title="Two-dimensional gel electrophoresis">2D gel electrophoresis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:75px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="998" data-file-width="459" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png/73px-Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png/110px-Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png/147px-Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png 2x" width="73"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coomassie_blue_stained_gel.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proteins stained on a PAGE gel using Coomassie blue dye.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Bradford_Assay">The Bradford Assay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Bradford Assay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Bradford_Assay" title="The Bradford Assay">The Bradford Assay</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Bradford_Assay" title="The Bradford Assay">The Bradford Assay</a> is a molecular biology technique which enables the fast, accurate quantitation of protein molecules utilizing the unique properties of a dye called <a href="/wiki/Coomassie_brilliant_blue" title="Coomassie brilliant blue">Coomassie Brilliant Blue</a> G-250.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[40]</a></sup> Coomassie Blue undergoes a visible color shift from reddish-brown to bright blue upon binding to protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[40]</a></sup> In its unstable, cationic state, Coomassie Blue has a background wavelength of 465 nm and gives off a reddish-brown color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-41">[41]</a></sup> When Coomassie Blue binds to protein in an acidic solution, the background wavelength shifts to 595 nm and the dye gives off a bright blue color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-41">[41]</a></sup> Proteins in the assay bind Coomassie blue in about 2 minutes, and the protein-dye complex is stable for about an hour, although it's recommended that absorbance readings are taken within 5 to 20 minutes of reaction initiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[40]</a></sup> The concentration of protein in the Bradford assay can then be measured using a visible light <a href="/wiki/Spectrophotometry" title="Spectrophotometry">spectrophotometer</a>, and therefore does not require extensive equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>This method was developed in 1975 by <a href="/wiki/Marion_M._Bradford" title="Marion M. Bradford">Marion M. Bradford</a>, and has enabled significantly faster, more accurate protein quantitation compared to previous methods: the Lowry procedure and the biuret assay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[40]</a></sup> Unlike the previous methods, the Bradford assay is not susceptible to interference by several non-protein molecules, including ethanol, sodium chloride, and magnesium chloride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[40]</a></sup>  However, it is susceptible to influence by strong alkaline buffering agents, such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_dodecyl_sulfate" title="Sodium dodecyl sulfate">sodium dodecyl sulfate</a> (SDS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-5"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macromolecule_blotting_and_probing">Macromolecule blotting and probing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Macromolecule blotting and probing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The terms <i>northern</i>, <i>western</i> and <i>eastern</i> blotting are derived from what initially was a molecular biology joke that played on the term <i><a href="/wiki/Southern_blot" title="Southern blot">Southern blotting</a></i>, after the technique described by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Southern" title="Edwin Southern">Edwin Southern</a> for the hybridisation of blotted DNA. Patricia Thomas, developer of the RNA blot which then became known as the <i>northern blot</i>, actually didn't use the term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_blotting">Southern blotting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Southern blotting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Southern_blot" title="Southern blot">Southern blot</a></div>
<p>Named after its inventor, biologist <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Southern" title="Edwin Southern">Edwin Southern</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_blot" title="Southern blot">Southern blot</a> is a method for probing for the presence of a specific DNA sequence within a DNA sample. DNA samples before or after <a href="/wiki/Restriction_enzyme" title="Restriction enzyme">restriction enzyme</a> (restriction endonuclease) digestion are separated by gel electrophoresis and then transferred to a membrane by blotting via <a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">capillary action</a>. The membrane is then exposed to a labeled DNA probe that has a complement base sequence to the sequence on the DNA of interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Southern blotting is less commonly used in laboratory science due to the capacity of other techniques, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PCR" title="PCR">PCR</a>, to detect specific DNA sequences from DNA samples. These blots are still used for some applications, however, such as measuring <a href="/wiki/Transgene" title="Transgene">transgene</a> copy number in <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">transgenic mice</a> or in the engineering of <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">gene knockout</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_cell_line" title="Stem cell line">embryonic stem cell lines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tian_2013_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tian_2013-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_blotting">Northern blotting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Northern blotting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_blot" title="Northern blot">Northern blot</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Northern_blot_diagram.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="998" data-file-width="1196" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Northern_blot_diagram.png/220px-Northern_blot_diagram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Northern_blot_diagram.png/330px-Northern_blot_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Northern_blot_diagram.png/440px-Northern_blot_diagram.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Northern_blot_diagram.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northern blot diagram</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Northern_blot" title="Northern blot">northern blot</a> is used to study the presence of specific RNA molecules as relative comparison among a set of different samples of RNA.  It is essentially a combination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denaturing_gel" title="Denaturing gel">denaturing RNA gel electrophoresis</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Blot_(biology)" title="Blot (biology)">blot</a>.  In this process RNA is separated based on size and is then transferred to a membrane that is then probed with a labeled <a href="/wiki/Complementarity_(molecular_biology)" title="Complementarity (molecular biology)">complement</a> of a sequence of interest. The results may be visualized through a variety of ways depending on the label used; however, most result in the revelation of bands representing the sizes of the RNA detected in sample.  The intensity of these bands is related to the amount of the target RNA in the samples analyzed.  The procedure is commonly used to study when and how much gene expression is occurring by measuring how much of that RNA is present in different samples, assuming that no post-transcriptional regulation occurs and that the levels of mRNA reflect proportional levels of the corresponding protein being produced.  It is one of the most basic tools for determining at what time, and under what conditions, certain genes are expressed in living tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_blotting">Western blotting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Western blotting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_blot" title="Western blot">Western blot</a></div>
<p>A western blot is a technique by which specific proteins can be detected from a mixture of proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-46">[46]</a></sup> Western blots can be used to determine the size of isolated proteins, as well as to quantify their expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Western_blot" title="Western blot">western blotting</a>, proteins are first separated by size, in a thin gel sandwiched between two glass plates in a technique known as <a href="/wiki/SDS-PAGE" title="SDS-PAGE">SDS-PAGE</a>. The proteins in the gel are then transferred to a <a href="/wiki/Polyvinylidene_fluoride" title="Polyvinylidene fluoride">polyvinylidene fluoride</a> (PVDF), nitrocellulose, nylon, or other support membrane. This membrane can then be probed with solutions of <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>. Antibodies that specifically bind to the protein of interest can then be visualized by a variety of techniques, including colored products, <a href="/wiki/Chemiluminescence" title="Chemiluminescence">chemiluminescence</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autoradiography" title="Autoradiography">autoradiography</a>. Often, the antibodies are labeled with enzymes. When a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemiluminescent" title="Chemiluminescent">chemiluminescent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substrate_(biochemistry)" title="Substrate (biochemistry)">substrate</a> is exposed to the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> it allows detection. Using western blotting techniques allows not only detection but also quantitative analysis. Analogous methods to western blotting can be used to directly stain specific proteins in live <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissue</a> sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_blotting">Eastern blotting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Eastern blotting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_blot" title="Eastern blot">Eastern blot</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_blot" title="Eastern blot">eastern blotting</a> technique is used to detect <a href="/wiki/Post-translational_modification" title="Post-translational modification">post-translational modification</a> of proteins. Proteins blotted on to the PVDF or nitrocellulose membrane are probed for modifications using specific substrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microarrays">Microarrays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Microarrays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/DNA_microarray" title="DNA microarray">DNA microarray</a></div>

codecs="vp9, opus"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Microarray_printing.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A DNA microarray being printed</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NA_hybrid.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="436" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NA_hybrid.svg/220px-NA_hybrid.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NA_hybrid.svg/330px-NA_hybrid.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NA_hybrid.svg/440px-NA_hybrid.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NA_hybrid.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hybridization of target to probe</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/DNA_microarray" title="DNA microarray">DNA microarray</a> is a collection of spots attached to a solid support such as a <a href="/wiki/Microscope_slide" title="Microscope slide">microscope slide</a> where each spot contains one or more single-stranded DNA <a href="/wiki/Oligonucleotide" title="Oligonucleotide">oligonucleotide</a> fragments. Arrays make it possible to put down large quantities of very small (100 micrometre diameter) spots on a single slide. Each spot has a DNA fragment molecule that is complementary to a single <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA sequence</a>. A variation of this technique allows the <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> of an organism at a particular stage in development to be qualified (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expression_profiling" title="Expression profiling">expression profiling</a>). In this technique the RNA in a tissue is isolated and converted to labeled <a href="/wiki/Complementary_DNA" title="Complementary DNA">complementary DNA</a> (cDNA). This cDNA is then hybridized to the fragments on the array and visualization of the hybridization can be done. Since multiple arrays can be made with exactly the same position of fragments, they are particularly useful for comparing the gene expression of two different tissues, such as a healthy and cancerous tissue. Also, one can measure what genes are expressed and how that expression changes with time or with other factors.
There are many different ways to fabricate microarrays; the most common are silicon chips, microscope slides with spots of ~100 micrometre diameter, custom arrays, and arrays with larger spots on porous membranes (macroarrays). There can be anywhere from 100 spots to more than 10,000 on a given array. Arrays can also be made with molecules other than DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allele-specific_oligonucleotide">Allele-specific oligonucleotide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Molecular_biology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Allele-specific oligonucleotide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allele-specific_oligonucleotide" title="Allele-specific oligonucleotide">Allele-specific oligonucleotide</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Allele-specific_oligonucleotide" title="Allele-specific oligonucleotide">Allele-specific oligonucleotide</a> (ASO) is a technique that allows detection of single base mutations without the need for PCR or gel electrophoresis. Short (20–25 nucleotides in length), labeled probes are exposed to the non-fragmented target DNA, hybridization occurs with high specificity due to the short length of the probes and even a single base change will hinder hybridization. The target DNA is then washed and the labeled probes that didn't hybridize are removed. The target DNA is then analyzed for the presence of the probe via radioactivity or fluorescence. In this experiment, as in most molecular biology techniques, a control must be used to ensure successful experimentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In molecular biology, procedures and technologies are continually being developed and older technologies abandoned. For example, before the advent of DNA <a href="/wiki/Gel_electrophoresis" title="Gel electrophoresis">gel electrophoresis</a> (<a href="/wiki/Agarose_gel_electrophoresis" title="Agarose gel electrophoresis">agarose</a> or <a href="/wiki/SDS-PAGE" title="SDS-PAGE">polyacrylamide</a>), the size of DNA molecules was typically determined by rate <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation" title="Sedimentation">sedimentation</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sucrose_gradient_centrifugation" title="Sucrose gradient centrifugation">sucrose gradients</a>, a slow and labor-intensive technique requiring expensive instrumentation; prior to sucrose gradients, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscometry" title="Viscometry">viscometry</a> was used. Aside from their historical interest, it is often worth knowing about older technology, as it is occasionally useful to solve another new problem for which the newer technique is inappropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
